0061452068|4.0|A rare book indeed amidst the torrent of tomes focusing on the familiar horsemen of the apocalypse, whether they are of climate change, peak oil, overpopulation, pandemics or our heading towards the singularity.<br /><br />The author draws his optimism from the well of accumulated human knowledge and ingenuity. He sees humankind's collective brain as a `problem solving machine' and leads the reader engagingly through the bumpy history of cultural, technological and economic development from earliest Palaeolithic trade and proto-agriculture to our present global economy. He shows with just the right amount of statistical and comparative data the main reasons for the astonishing material betterment enjoyed by the masses today in relation to the times of the Sun King Louis XIV, let alone compared to earlier epochs. He shows how trade contributed to the fertility of ideas, how innovation, specialization, mechanization, division of labour, and so forth, coupled with free exchange of ideas and unfettered entrepreneurship are the foundations which make prosperity possible for the greatest number of people. He is highly critical of ideologies promoting self-sufficiency or top-down engineering, whether social or otherwise, and of those wanting to freeze technologies at a certain level, be they in agriculture or elsewhere. He also demonstrates clearly that all the regularly predicted `turning points' or thresholds, supposedly leading to imminent catastrophe in the past, were proven wrong each time and concludes that this will likely be true for the future too.<br /><br />As long as Matt Ridley stays with a historical narrative, he is on firm ground, and his book could be seen as a good riposte to economist Jeff Rubin, whose \\"Why Your World Is About To Get A Whole Lot Smaller\\" singles out peak-oil as the harbinger of severe economic adjustments. While Rubin's focus is in my opinion too narrow (see my respective Amazon book review), Ridley's treatment of what he calls \\"The Great Fears of Today\\" - Africa, Pandemics, Overpopulation, and Climate Change - strikes me as being too nonchalant. He posits that they are either not as significant as they are made out to be, or that they will be overcome with the usual human ingenuity, as have been other challenges in the past. And what is the basis for his confidence? - The simple extrapolation of the technological and economic success story since the beginning of the Industrial Revolution!<br /><br />The development of the highly technological society and socio-economic environment we have created and live in today is an all too short phenomenon in the human experience and should therefore raise serious questions about it being a valid yard stick for extrapolation into the future. After all, even his avowed optimism does not prevent the author from mentioning a few times, that decline and later disintegration of sophisticated societies and economic systems back into simple economies of self reliance and subsistence for reasons of politics, religion, pandemics, resource exhaustion or local climate change has been observed throughout history. So it could happen again, only on a much larger scale and with more dire consequences, considering today's global interconnectedness and large populations. And that is precisely where the Neo-Malthusians and other doomsayers come in.<br /><br />Although relegated by him to the margins, Matt Ridley's remarks nevertheless acknowledge that progress of the human species has never been, nor will it likely be in the future, a continuous \\"bull market\\". \\"Rational Optimism\\" should perhaps be rephrased to \\"Cautious Optimism\\" and elaborate a bit more on the inevitable bumps, washouts and landslides to be expected on the road ahead, especially since his timeline is only the next 100 years. Either way, this book is well worth reading and pondering about - a scholarly work covering a wide scope and written in very accessible prose.

0385534639|4.0|Being on many \\"best of 2011\\" lists, The Night Circus has big shoes to fill for potential readers. For the most part, I thought The Night Circus lived up to the hype; it had an imaginative, whimsical quality about it that was engaging and refreshing. Some complain about the flowery prose, but I thought it added to the story's magical mood and ambience, the poetic descriptiveness mirroring the fanciful event that is the circus. The allusions to Shakespearean plays (the forbidden love between rivals, Prospero, etc) are a nice touch as well. Morgenstern uses the circus itself as her main protagonist, as components--time, people, events--revolve around it and are intertwined together.<br /><br />Several of the \\"other\\" main characters were a mixed bag. Celia and Marco--the key characters in the \\"bet\\" between the two magicians-- weren't as interesting to me as some others, maybe because I thought their \\"love\\" for each other was forced down our throats a little too much. Another thing that I found disappointing was simply that the key magicians in the novel (Marco, Celia, and Prospero) almost lacked human elements, coming across more like mythical puppeteers pulling strings and controlling things behind the scenes. The vignettes focusing on Bailey's fascination with the circus were the highlight of the novel; Morgenstern really shines connecting his story with the players in the circus. I also enjoyed the stories about the twins, Poppet and Widget, and their connection to the circus.<br /><br />While The Night Circus is impressive mostly, one slight annoyance is that the limitations of magic are never clearly determined, some illusions too far over-the-top. It's a book about magic and illusion, yes, but some parts strain believability (Marco changing his physical appearance on a whim, Prospero making his physical form vanish, etc). I thought most of the plot was relatively well-conceived, but then, towards the end, some of it was too hard to swallow. As a result, the endingwas a bit of a letdown.<br /><br />However, The Night Circus is an impressive debut for the author. Morgenstern has an effective way of building atmosphere with each of the various stories, with the circus its central figure. In this way, it is less about Marco or Celia or the contest they are involved in, and more about the existence of the circus through time.

0465043577|3.0|This book aims to introduce investors to fractal analysis. The main idea that Mandelbrot offers is that the markets are inherently more risky than conventional finance theory would have us believe. The principal reason for that is that the markets do not follow the classical random walk based on a normal distribution but are subject to much more violent perturbations based on the power law. As a result, modern portfolio theory and option pricing theory are wrong because they misjudge risk.<br /><br />While all of the above seems correct and there are no flaws in Mandelbrot's logic, the main flaw of the book is that it only looks at price movements themselves when building those fractal models. Fractals are a beautiful mathematical abstraction that seems to describe how markets move quite well. However, prices are not moved by previous prices (at least not to a significant extent) but by changes in the incoming information and in the investors' sentiment. In other words, prices are moved by fundamentals or changes in investors' expectations about fundamentals. Mandelbrot does not even attempt to explain how fundamentals affect asset prices - his sole focus in on price changes themselves. This fact makes this book an interesting read for a curious mind but not a guide for a practical investor.

Having been home-schooled previously, it's his first time in a public school setting where he has to interact with other kids.  This is difficult enough for a child who isn't disfigured, so imagine how hard it is for Auggie.<br /><br />Auggie has the support and love of his parents and older sister, but he longs to be \\"normal.\\"  The author, R.J. Palacio, presents a believable story of August's first year in a public school setting as he interacts with other students; some kind, some not-so-kind.  Palacio presents an accurate depiction of kids; their attitudes, thoughts, and feelings; and that longing to be popular and fit in.<br /><br />The book starts out from Auggie's sensitive point of view as he tells his story.  I was really enjoying reading the first 75 pages of the book when the author did something that I typically dislike and has become too commonplace: she changed the story's point of view (POV).  Now we're viewing the story through Auggie's sister's eyes.  But it doesn't stop there.  The POV changes several more times before coming back to Auggie.  That's when I discovered that this storytelling device actually worked for this particular novel.  Each change of POV filled in the blanks from the previous stories and POVs, and then moved the overall story along.<br /><br />Book don't typically elicit a lot of emotion for me like seeing the story up on the big screen does.  However, with WONDER, I have to admit that I occasionally felt tears of happiness at the kindness expressed by people around Auggie, as well as sadness during the rough spots.  The story was really sweet and it has a fantastic message.  Although this book is marketed for \\"young adult,\\" it's a great read for all ages.  It teaches manners, respect, appreciation, and, most importantly, kindness.  It's relatable for anyone who felt like they didn't fit in.  I highly recommend buying and sharing this book.<br /><br />P.S. Indeed, they are one of the few indie-dance bands that have made it into the top 40 charts.<br /><br />The first four tracks - \\"Helena Beat\\", \\"Pumped Up Kicks\\", \\"Call It What You Want\\", \\"Don't Stop (Color On The Wall)\\", as well as \\"Houdini\\", are the strongest five songs on the album. Unless you've living in a cave, you've heard their biggest hit \\"Pumped Up Kicks\\" by now.  Like \\"Pumped Up Kicks\\", the other singles also have hard-hitting percussion (electric and real) that you can bop your head to or dance to. \\"Helena Beat\\" and \\"Houdini\\" are my favorite songs from the album.<br /><br />The other songs on the album are decent as well, and blend in well with the stronger songs. They are somewhat slower and less cheery than the above-mentioned tracks. My only complaint with the album overall is that every song has similar elements. 0307352145|5.0|Our culture does not appreciate quiet. We tend to equate social power with social prowess. The outspoken people seen are as the leaders in our culture. Susan Cain, however, seeks to dispel this notion that people with a quiet nature cannot be major influencers or leaders. She opens her book Quiet by discussing one of the most influential figures in the civil rights movement. Rosa Parks made an impact in the civil rights movement through her quietness. She chose to take a quiet stand instead of being outspoken. In doing so, she became a symbol of strength in the movement. This is the first of many examples of introverts that Cain offers to show the power of quiet in a world that likes to talk.<br /><br />Quiet is Cain's attempt to prove that, despite America's preference to extroversion, introverts can be extremely successful and influential in our culture. She begins by discussing the \\"Extroverted Ideal.\\" Since the time that Andrew Carnegie taught us how to win friends and influence people, the ideal personality has be extroverted. In the world of business and leadership, the way to get ahead is to speak up and stand out. Cain visits Harvard Business School to understand how they teach students to succeed in business. It becomes very clear that Harvard teaches their students to speak up or they won't succeed. The issue that Cain finds, however, is that in all their talking, people aren't listening. In this first part of the book, Cain poses the question of whether or not the extroverted ideal is really ideal. What she finds is people being trained to talk the most, whether they are educated or not, in order to get ahead.  Businesses promote group brainstorming which tends to favor the outspoken. Cain instead shows that often people who quietly, by themselves, and are allowed to think through the problems are more productive and effective that people working together in groups where people are fighting for attention. While the outspoken standout, they are not always bringing success and are often times talking over those who can give good ideas.<br /><br />The next part of here book looks at the biology and psychology behind introversion. Cain seeks to discover if introversion genetic. This section is much different that the first part of the book, but still interesting. I found this section of the book personally interesting because I read through it going, \\"Oh, That's why I am like that.\\" Introversion and extroversion seems to be built into our brain functions even from birth. It effects the way we think and process information.<br /><br />The third part of Cain's book compares our culture with Asian cultures to see if the Extroverted ideal is apparent in all cultures. Cain shows that there are major differences between the two cultures. America sees extroversion as ideal, while Asian cultures see introversion as ideal. One of the primary differences is how success is achieved. Introverted cultures tend to succeed through persistence and perseverance, while extroverted cultures succeed but speaking over others and getting your ideas heard. Cain uses this to show the some of the people we see as successful succeeded because of persistence instead of outspokenness.<br /><br />Last, Cain discusses living as an introvert and with introverts. She applies what she's discussed in previous sections to having relationships, working in an extroverted culture, and raising introverted children. This section is a good balance to the rest of the book which is trying to show that extroversion is not the ideal. Cain reminds her readers that sometimes introverts do need to speak up, but should also not lose the good qualities that introversion brings. She also offers very good practical information on how to live in introvert-extrovert relationships and how to raise introvert children.<br /><br />Quiet: The Power of Introverts in a World that Can't Stop Talking is a great book. Susan Cain does a good job of presenting how our culture favors extroversion and looks down on introverts. It may seem at first that Cain is trying to say that introverts are better than extroverts, but reading through the whole work she does a wonderful job of challenging introverts to grow in certain areas. She also challenges extroverts to consider the benefits that introverts are able bring. As an introvert myself I most appreciated the second and fourth sections of the book. I was very interested to learn about myself and why I am the way I am. The second section does a good job of explaining many the biological factors of introversion and extroversion. The fourth section is very helpful because of the helpful advice for living with extroverts and raising and teaching introverted children.<br /><br />I would recommend this book to just about anyone. If you are an introverted, you will find this book very helpful and encouraging. If you are an extrovert, you can learn why your introverted friends are the way they are and how to best interact with them. I would highly recommend this book to anyone in a business situation, this book can be really helpful in learning how to get the most out of the introverts in your office. I would also recommend it to my friends in the ministry along with Adam S. McHugh's book [[ASIN:0830837027 Introverts in the Church: Finding Our Place in an Extroverted Culture]]. I hope that Cain's book will help people change their understanding of introversion. Introversion is not a weakness. There may be areas where introverts are weak, but there are always areas where introverts tend to be stronger than extroverts.<br /><br />I received this book for free from WaterBrook Multnomah Publishing Group for this review. I was not required to write a positive review. The opinions I have expressed are my own.xtroversion and looks down on introverts. It may seem at first that Cain is trying to say that introverts are better than extroverts, but reading through the whole work she does a wonderful job of challenging introverts to grow in certain areas. I cannot remember ever buying a  book after reading a library copy, except when our book discussion group later decided to use it.<br /><br />Pinker correctly says that this analysis is \\"unsentimental history\\" based on \\"statistical literacy.\\"<br /><br />A great strength of this work is Pinker's extensive and persuasive documentation of how violent and nasty life in previous centuries  was.  The massive torture of people and animals in the past, which Pinker does not shrink from describing in gory detail, is sobering and depressing, but very educational.<br /><br />Pinker admits that we are far from having arrived at utopia.  But  a number of graphs illustrate how the chances of being murdered or raped over the long haul have been greatly reduced from one century to another,  and even the likelihood (expressed as a percentage of the current populations) of being killed in wars has gone way down.<br /><br />The author capably explores a number of possible explanations for the improvements, accepting some and rejecting others.<br /><br />Although Pinker's thesis is very upbeat, he pointedly refrains from arguing that the trends toward improvement he documents will inevitably continue.  He says our appropriate attitude should be \\"gratitude\\" rather than \\"optimism.\\"<br /><br />This book would make a wonderful basis for reading and discussion in college political science classes, and its 700 pages (before footnotes kick in)  would probably mean it would have to be the main text for a one-semester undergraduate class.

B005LAJ23A|5.0|When former showrunner Frank Darabont \\"left\\" THE WALKING DEAD before the start of the second season, I was extremely worried for its future. It seemed to be going in a very positive direction after only a few episodes in its first season, and much of that seemed to be Darabont's passion for the project as well as creator Robert Kirkman's desire to not slavishly adhere to his own source material. That was a surprising high point for the first season, since the comic itself is so amazingly good, and has several fans, that he would be more than willing to depart from his own material in order to keep audiences on their toes.<br /><br />It was even rumored that a major cast member was ready to walk off set since Darabont's leaving, but who it was was never confirmed, and it certainly didn't happen.<br /><br />But when the show came back for its first episode of the second season, it made a point of adhering to certain major plot points of the comic while keeping the relationships and characters fresh, and with Glen Mazzara as the new showrunner, it seemed like the show was going to keep going along with all of the forward momentum it had gained from the previous season. However, I had some concerns about the bonafides of Mazzara. He had been an Exec Producer on several disappointing TV series, like \\"Life\\", \\"Crash\\" and \\"HawthoRNe\\", but he was also an exec on one of the best cop shows ever, \\"The Shield\\". So, with any work on \\"The Shield\\" cancelling any of the poorer parts of his career out, it was looking like smooth scary sailing for this season.<br /><br />Now, it's worth pointing out that THE WALKING DEAD has easily become the most contentious piece of genre television since SyFy's reboot of BATTLESTAR GALACTICA. If you look at the internet after an episode, it's aflutter with conversations proclaiming it either one of the worst shows ever or one of the best shows ever. It's also worth mentioning that neither side is right. When an episode of TWD is good, it ranks amongst some of the very best TV has to offer. Episodes like \\"Chupacabra\\" where Daryl is stranded in the wilderness, \\"Pretty Much Dead Already\\" when the group opens the barn, and \\"Better Angels\\" as Shane enacts some of his solutions to some of his problems are some of the best episodes of TV in recent memory. But further more, there is another group of viewers that sound off even more frequently than either other group: The \\"I'm Watching Because I Want It To Get Better\\" group. This is the group with the most complaints, and in my opinion, the ones with the most validity to their claims. They want the show to have tighter writing. They want the characters not to be glossed over. They don't want too many 'Red Shirts' on the show (referring of course to the old \\"Star Trek\\" and how the nameless characters existed merely to get killed in an episode). They want the show to keep moving. There are moments that I agree with all of these statements, but I'm seeing what the show is doing (or trying to do) and am much more willing to forgive the show's occasional plot hole or deus ex machina or the \\"This Character is About to Do Something REALLY Stupid\\" moments in favor of the work being done by the cast, crew and creative staff.<br /><br />However, it's far from perfect when plot and characters start to get bogged down from over-expository moments to a particular episode. Characters like Lori, Dale, Carol and ESPECIALLY T-Dog are essentially lost in the character arc shuffle despite the major things happen (Carol and Sophia, Lori and The Surprise, Dale and his staunch sense of humanity, and T-Dog and his... well... he did have that arm injury... yes, I'm essentially calling T-Dog a useless character bordering on being the \\"Token Black Stereotype\\"). However, when there are moments of their arcs moving forward, they do so with tremendous momentum.<br /><br />But even with Rick struggling to lead the group as a paragon of virtue, Andrea overcoming her personal demons and becoming a strong element to the group's assault force, Carl spending formative moments in the midst of far too many moral grey areas for a child to have to experience, the new character of Herschel and his staunch religious and moral beliefs and then having those beliefs shattered, and Glenn and his discovery of his more masculine side with the help of new character Maggie... this season belonged to one character: Shane. If there is a character that we can identify with the most AND the least, it's Shane. Here's a man willing to do anything to keep himself safe as well as the woman and child he loves. He also is willing to commit to an almost morally bankrupt existence so that he can do those things. Jon Bernthal proves to be the most versatile cast member for this season, and his arc is the most compelling. If Bernthal doesn't get an Emmy nod for his work on this season, it honestly won't make sense.<br /><br />Speaking of the cast, there are several who shine: Jeffrey DeMunn as the ever-watchful ever-wise Dale, Laurie Holden as the struggling-to-find-her-place Andrea, Steven Yeun as the occasionally-wisecracking and romantically-challenged Glenn, Andrew Lincoln as the constantly-conflicted Rick, but the other two strongest performances besides Bernthal are Scott Wilson as Herschel and Norman Reedus as Daryl Dixon. Sadly, the weakest link to the cast is Sarah Wayne Callies as Lori. She didn't impress during her run on the show PRISON BREAK, and just seems too far out of her depth since most of her scenes are with other, better performances. Also sad are the criticisms of Chandler Riggs playing young Carl. He's actually not out of his depth here, and his character is that of a young boy who is trying to maintain some of his youthful nature despite the omnipresent death that looms around him in either physical or metaphysical form. He's reckless, impatient, irresponsible, and sometimes flat-out dumb. But surprisingly, most kids that age are like that, and I don't think that the character or the young actor deserves the criticism he gets.<br /><br />From a technical standpoint, the effects work on the show is flawless. Greg Nicotero, who also transitioned into directing during this season, has supervised all the VFX and makeup and has done so with an expert eye for detail, even if some of that detail is EXTREMELY gory. Other directors like Ernest Dickerson, who's directed several episodes of this show, as well as many other shows such as BURN NOTICE, DEXTER, TREME, and most especially THE WIRE (not arguably the greatest cop show ever to hit television), shows a great eye for striking visuals as well as being a very good actor's director, which was especially proven in his direction of the season finale, \\"Beside The Dying Fire\\". Other strong creative forces kept proving themselves time and again this season, such as writer/co-producer Evan Reilly, who came from another favorite show of mine, RESCUE ME, and Kirkman himself who did his share of heavy lifting in the writing and exec producing this season, despite being the writer of at least three monthly comic books as well.<br /><br />The only problem I have with the show consistently is the same problem I have with all action or genre-oriented entertainment: The unerring shootist or unlimited ammo UNLESS the moment requires them to run out of ammo, or miss to create tension or suspense. You see, characters walk around this show like Wild Bill Hickok in a John Woo film. Sure, there were moments when Shane and Rick taught the members of the group how to shoot, but BELIEVE ME, you still can't shoot a zombie in the head hanging out of a moving car no matter how many months you might have been practicing, and you can't fire 20-odd rounds of a 7-round shotgun. The only time the people miss or run out of ammo is when the story requires it, like reloading with a Walker creeping up behind you, or just grazing the skull of a fellow group member when if it had been a Walker, it would be a completely perfect headshot.<br /><br />The most common complaint I hear about this show is the lack of Walkers. That is one of those complaints that I dismiss entirely out of hand. You want constant zombie presence and constant zombie killing, watch the RESIDENT EVIL films. THE WALKING DEAD is not just a zombie show, much in the same way that BATTLESTAR GALACTICA wasn't just a space-battle show. The characters have to interact and grow, especially in an environment such as this where society no longer exists other than in their own memories. Morality is something that appears to be the relic of a bygone era, and this is a land that seems to be ruled strictly by Darwinian principles. Concepts of good and evil have become luxuries, and the new law is survival. Some members of this group believe that very strongly, and some are shouting to the heavens that there must be a better way to live other than just survival. The argument could honestly be made either way, and more often than not, those are the moments when the show can either be at its best or it can wither away any momentum it had. The show is already great, but finding a balance between words and deeds will be how the show will live or die, but there are far too many smart and talented people behind this program for that to happen. The show has only had 19 episodes. Young shows will have growing pains, but when I rewatched the season finale, there were too many things that showed even more promise for the new season.character or the young actor deserves the criticism he gets.<br /><br />From a technical standpoint, the effects work on the show is flawless. 0385344228|5.0|Defending Jacob is an excellent courtroom drama and murder mystery. The details of the court case will totally capture your interest, and at times you will believe the characters are real and want to scream at the lawyers because of the way they manipulate the facts, witnesses and the jury.<br />How far would a parent go to protect a child accused of murder? Would parents actually suspect their own child? Could they believe their child was capable of such an act? Would friends remain loyal after such an accusation? How does a family survive the isolation and humiliation of the trial and all it encompasses?<br />Anyone who reads this book will be left with the thought, what would I have done, how would I cope in each of the situations, with my friends if their child was accused, with my son and my spouse if my child were accused? Could I hold up under such embarrassing scrutiny and stress?<br />This story takes place in Newton, a bedroom community of Boston. It begins in a Grand Jury courtroom as Mr. Andrew Barber, a former District Attorney, is on the stand being examined by a fellow lawyer regarding his son's murder charge. It then seems to proceed backwards in time, to the trial of his son, Jacob, for the murder of a teenage bully, Ben Rifkin. Throughout the novel, interspersed with the dialogue, there are vignettes from the Grand Jury hearing to fill in missing pieces of the story. You are there with the accused and almost feel like a jury member yourself, listening to the facts as they are told, wondering about Jacob's guilt or innocence, judging the performances of the lawyers and the innuendos about the accused.<br />Jacob might be described as a sullen boy, not social, a geek, emotionally immature, outside the social strata of the mainstream high school student. His reactions are not quite right, not what one would expect, in certain circumstances. When he is implicated in the murder of a fellow student, the story explodes into the public eye. His innocence is never really a factor; his guilt is all anyone ever envisions. Jacob's demeanor makes people believe he could have done it, and circumstantial evidence points in his direction. All the details of his and his family's life are examined and made public.<br />Did the investigators properly investigate the case, looking for all suspects, or did they simply aim at Jacob, disregarding other evidence in order to solve the crime expeditiously because of its horrific nature? He is emotionally immature. His ancestors have committed crimes of horrific violence. Is there, therefore, a murder gene or a propensity to commit violent crimes?<br />Friends can make incriminating statements about the accused that would seem innocent in other circumstances. The problem of putting anything on the internet, which becomes public as soon as you are under suspicion, is really illuminated. There is no place to hide if you have implicated yourself on any of the public social sites. Any stupid thing you may have done will cast suspicion upon you because if you are the accused, the benefit of the doubt seems to disappear and is given to your accuser. Every thing you do will be examined six ways to Sunday in the media, by those looking to make a headline and a name for themselves; what happens to you and your family, in the event of your innocence, is immaterial to them. You and your family will be tormented and no one will care. The Barbers had been respected members of the community, yet they fell from grace immediately.<br />The novel shows how easy it is for the prosecutor and the defense attorney to skew the evidence in their favor. The court system and investigatory methods are subject to so much corruption. Evidence that might help the defendant is hidden, just to prove a point and win the case, even when that evidence might shed light on the truth. Winning is the main goal.<br />This novel expertly examines the dynamic of family interaction, social relationships, marriage, genetic background, the psychology of motive and crime, the influence of circumstantial evidence, the damage an inference can cause, the playacting and manipulation on the part of lawyers, and it exposes the strain a tragic event puts on love and friendship.<br />The author has fully developed his characters, exposing their flaws and their strengths. He exposes the corruption in our legal system: the dog eat dog world of the DA's office, the motivation of politics which further corrupts the system, the danger of a media out of control, the difficulty of determining the truth. The novel feels so true to life that you can imagine it really happening. The ending, is totally unexpected so don't peek!

1451683405|5.0|A wonderful book that advances the counter-intuitive idea that free will is an illusion. This book will garner some negative opinions because it contradicts humanity's common sense view that each person is a completely free agent.  And nowhere on Earth is this view held more sacred than in the United States with its history of rugged individualism and personal responsibility.  This makes Sam Harris' *Free Will* all the more important.<br /><br />At less than 100 pages it seems a bit silly to refer to *Free Will* as an enormously important read, but it wouldn't be silly in the slightest to say so.  *Free Will* explains why we think we have free will and why that conception of our agency is wrong.  I can think of nothing more central to the way we live our lives than this mistaken belief.  Fortunately, changing our understanding of free will wouldn't change the way we live our lives except in important subtle ways, and Harris explains this in his book as well.<br /><br />I would also recommended reading Malcolm Gladwell's *Outliers*, which brings Harris' argument to life with deftly told stories that amuse as much as they inform.

0060873027|5.0|As I write this review on March 24 2012, there are 30 x 5-star reviews vs. 81 x 1-star reviews. The number of negative reviews and the fairly low ratings averaging 2.5 stars out of 5 stars nearly convinced me to skip Michael Crichton's last novel. Nearly. I am so glad I decided to give it a chance as I enjoyed it as much as all his other novels with the exception of \\"Airframe\\". I had recently re-read \\"Jurassic Park\\" which I found as thrilling as the first time I read it many years ago. And I couldn't help comparing \\"Micro\\" to \\"Jurassic Park\\".<br />With 534 x 5-star reviews, \\"Jurassic Park\\" still managed to receive 17 x 1-star reviews which baffles me. Some of the critics are: no literary flair, hollow and pointless, poor character description, and so on. These are, of course, very similar to the critics of \\"Micro\\": poorly written, ridiculous, insipid, terrible dialogs, predictable...<br /><br />I haven't read all of Michael Crichton's books, not the earlier ones anyway, but it seems to me that Crichton liked to write stories that were fast paced (Jurassic Park, Prey, Timeline), set in an unusual setting, and that he had a particular penchant for technology with a science-fiction bend. No doubt, as with H.G. Wells, some of his predictions will come to pass. Anyway, if readers want character development, why read Crichton? Character development was never his forte. You might be better off reading \\"War and Peace\\" by Leo Tolstoy. Lots of action with excellent character development. Or Jane Austen. Another writer gifted at character development.<br />As for literary flair, I recommend \\"To Kill a Mockingbird\\" by Harper Lee...<br />In \\"Micro\\", there are a few grad students working together, there is the required villain and his hapless assistant, each is introduced to the reader, we learn more about them as the book progresses, but it's really not the point. There was enough information to satisfy me and I can be as critical as the next person. The point of the book was the interaction of man and beast if you will... What happens when a civilized person is thrown in the middle of the wild confronted to the beast. The world is unforgiving, has no pity and no remorse. \\"Micro\\" was a twist on the nature of the wild. We don't think much of the micro world other than to lament at the pesky bugs in summertime.<br />Regarding credibility, nanotechnology was already previously explored by Crichton in \\"Prey\\" written a decade ago.<br /><br />I guess there was a lot of attitude towards this last endeavor by Crichton -because- it is suspected that he did not write it, or did not write much of it, and certainly did not finish it. What if he didn't? I was forewarned and wondered if I could work out which sections were Crichton's and which were Preston's. I couldn't tell. The style was in my humble opinion seamless, as was the direction of the story though I will admit one turn of event did surprise me.<br />I have only read one book by Richard Preston, The Cobra Event, and actually liked it, ghastly though it was. I suspect that if \\"Micro\\" had been published under Michael Crichton's name with no mention of Richard Preston, the book would have been another success.<br />\\"Micro\\" was certainly informative enough as were most of Crichton's books. I learned a great deal about the micro world and insects. Yes, I was reminded at some point of the family movie \\"Honey, I shrunk the kids\\", but the parallel quickly ended as what followed was far from cute and the micro world proved as unforgiving as the Amazon jungle would prove to anyone who happened to be dropped in the middle of it with nothing to help ensure survival.<br />Noone will be convinced by my review who has a mind set against \\"Micro\\". It still remains an entertaining, fast-paced, enjoyable, informative novel that unfortunately concludes Michael Crichton's literary career. Well done, Mr. Crichton, you will be greatly missed.

0439023513|1.0|This train wreck of a finale (which made me wish I'd just skipped the ENTIRE triology--yes, *that* bad) was obviously the sad result of a money-hungry publisher rushing Collins to deliver on the third book asap...and Collins lacking the integrity to demand more time. Sadly, the hoopla of the trilogy is perpetuated by excited readers in who've just read the first or second book telling all their friends to read this Best! Trilogy! Ever! all before they get to this final disappointment of a book.<br /><br />Those of us 'detracting' aren't upset about the not-happy ending. Hello, this is a dystopian book about people killing each other. What we wanted was a well-constructed story that is somewhat satisfying. Which this is far from. The book reads like an unedited first-draft about to the torn to shreds in a freshman creative writing workshop. Why? Everything falls apart. Not only is the writing abysmal; the characters become these shoddily slapped-together stick figures who do things for no reason at all, disappear in a poof of exploding/chomped-on flesh, or just become wearisome in their psychotic inner-babble (that would be Katniss).<br /><br />The story fails in all the ways that it previously succeeded in the previous books (characters you care about, complex conflicts, stakes that matter, etc etc). Note that the readers who defend the book are generally adults who wax on about how war is hell and we have to accept realism, blah blah blah. But it isn't about whether we can 'handle' a depressing conclusion or not. It's about why we read stories in the first place--not just nonfiction. Stories have to matter, have to make us care. They aren't supposed to be didactic or manipulative. We don't come to them to get schooled on things. And even if this one was written with the main [lame] goal of making a point about society and war and crap, then it still could've been done in a well-crafted way that mattered a bit more. B005LAIH3G|4.0|Like a lot of folks, I read the John Carter series as a kid.  Then I read them again as a young adult.  And I re-read them again a few years ago (I'm now in my 50's).  I'd seen the lame attempts at trying to bring ERB's John Carter to the silver screen and kept wondering \\"When is a real studio going to take interest in this?\\"<br /><br />And finally, a \\"real\\" studio (Disney) has stepped up to the plate and brought the world a worthy adaptation of ERB's John Carter saga.<br /><br />Is it a perfect adaptation?  Nah...<br /><br />But it is pretty close.<br /><br />What didn't work for me:<br /><br />* Many of the longer shots were too tightly framed.  Barsoom needed a sense of greater space.<br />* Taylor Kitsch has adequate acting chops, but the character of John Carter is a hard man.  IMHO, TK just didn't have the gravitas to pull it off effectively.  He was pretty, but I just didn't see hard-won military experience and intense emotional loss in his face.  (This leaves me wondering why the director didn't consider either Mark Strong, Willem Dafoe, or another, more life-experienced actor, since they would have been more effective in portraying JC than Kitsch.  Kitsch's JC really needed a few more miles on him.)<br />* Barsoom was entirely too bright (minor physics queep, I know, but the director/editors should have toned down the day shots to better approximate the actual Mars).<br />* The bromance between Tars Tarkas and JC seemed heavily downplayed.  In the books, these two were fast friends and reliable battle partners.  It was there in this movie, but seemed to be brushed off to the side a bit.<br />* The lead-in segment was 5-10 minutes too long.  It needed to be edited down. (Although I did enjoy the humorous bits.)<br /><br />What did work:<br /><br />* The story: While an obvious blending of different parts of the first two books, as an adaptation, it hung together pretty well.<br />* The technical execution and art direction were first-rate-bordering-on-brilliant, especially the airships.  The other sets, props, and even the Tharks, all had that definite Barsoomian something.  Woola was awesome.<br />* Lynn Collins is the embodiment on Earth of Dejah Thoris in all of her smoldering, fierce, egg-laying glory.<br />* The end segment.  Shorter and more effective at getting JC back to Barsoom.<br /><br />Overall, I thought it was okay, but it just didn't invoke the sense of alien awesomeness that the books do.  I'll definitely buy the BD when it comes out (especially if it has an additional 46 minutes of footage featuring more Dejah...)  I'd definitely like to see a sequel though, to see if the director can round things out a bit more and maybe bring a darker, more adult sensibility to life on Barsoom.  A little less swash and a little more buckle, I'm thinking...<br /><br />BTW, folks that insist on comparing this to Star Wars, Avatar, et al. may certainly be correct in comparing film-to-film.  As for who's deriving from whom though, they should keep in mind that ERB wrote his JC series 100 years ago.  And it's highly unlikely that Star Wars, Avatar, or what-have-you, would even exist in their present forms if ERB's John Carter series had not been written to inspire those stories' creators.

B006QVRUS2|4.0|Roman Polanski's CARNAGE is a savage little film that desires nothing less than to undercut both marriage and parenthood.  It revolves around two couples: uptight Penelope (Jodie Foster) and annoyingly placating Michael (John C. Reilly), and tightly-wound Nancy (Kate Winslet) and distracted business man Alan (Cristoph Waltz).  They've met to discuss a fight between their two children, and immediately the differences between the couples become glaring.  As the film progresses, the couples turn on themselves, so that eventually they're identifying with each other by criticizing their own spouses.<br /><br />It's a bleak film.  No violence, no gore, minimal cursing, but it's still a bleak, bitter film.  And yet immensely hilarious; Polanski and co-writer (and original playwright) Yasmina Reza have a truly twisted sense of humor, and find a great joke in the most disturbing situations. (When Penelope exclaims, \\"Your son brutalized our son!\\", Alan rejoins, \\"They're young!\\")  The four leads are admirable; it's nice to see Foster deliver a knockout performance again; Reilly gets another chance, after his rise to fame as a comedy actor, to go back to his dramatic chops; and Winslet and Waltz are at their primal best.<br /><br />And that's it.  Four great actors, one room, and a hallway.  There's humor, and there's tension, and at the center is a debate about parental blame: maybe your kids aren't messed up, maybe it's you.  CARNAGE isn't a perfect movie (the female characters are a bit too similar at times, and the ending comes rather abruptly, with only a feeble attempt at resolution), but it's certainly a good one, and earns its place among Polanskis' better (if maybe not best) films.

B006ZCWTV0|4.0|On his latest offering [[ASIN:B007F39498 Wrecking Ball]], Bruce Springsteen hits a `home run.' Wrecking Ball contains a set of eleven consistent cuts that speak to the recession-affected individuals containing relevant messages of hope and the unrelenting resolve to become stronger.  Easily Bruce's best recent offering, there are few rubs characterizing Wrecking Ball, making it one of 2012's crowning achievements as far as album releases are concerned.  Bruce, as well as Ron Aniello, serve as producers on this solid effort.<br /><br />The album explodes with the pummeling drums and simplistic harmonic progression of \\"We Take Care of Our Own,\\" which benefits from a nice, straight ahead, traditional rock and roll sound.  The evolution and gradual intensification of this cut are selling points, not to mention a well penned refrain: \\"We take care of our own...wherever this flag's flown/we take care of our own.\\" \\"Easy Money\\" loses no momentum, sporting a signature Springsteen-esque folk-rock feel.  Much like \\"We Take Care of Our Own,\\" Springsteen's songwriting and pacing is focused on the evolution and build-up of a song, making \\"Easy Money\\" another superb moment.  The background vocals on neutral syllables is a high point, not to mention the use of violin, acoustic piano, and organ.  Springsteen is certainly on his `game.'<br /><br />\\"Shackled and Drawn\\" is clever if for no other reason than containing elements of  James Brown's \\"Me and My Baby Got Our Own Thing Going.\\" Springsteen keeps the folk-rock groove intact and focuses on acoustic, organic sounds (rhythmic acoustic guitar, etc.). Springsteen's vocals are soulful and gritty, the background vocals enhance and accentuate, and the songwriting is easy-going, namely on the incredibly catchy refrain: \\"Shackled and drawn, shackled and drawn/pick up the rock son, carry it on...\\" \\"Jack of All Trades\\" trumps its predecessor, adding Tom Morello on guitar.  The cut opens mysteriously and ominously and contrasts the pace of the three previous cuts, opting for a slower tempo.  Bruce definitely channels Bob Dylan here, in relation to songwriting and his approach to the performance (the influence shows). Again, pacing is a high point, particular as more instruments and elements are added to the superb arrangement including horns and strings.<br /><br />\\"Death To My Hometown\\" cedes nothing and contains elements of \\"The Last Words of Copernicus.\\"  The groove is optimistic and pounding, contrasting the solemnity that characterized \\"Jack of All Trades.\\"  The pummeling groove here gives Springsteen some `hip-hop credibility' to say the least, given the big, robust nature of the drums.  Who would've ever referenced Springsteen and hip-hop right? \\"The Depression\\" again taps Tom Morello for his guitar talents and has some particularly nice soloing.  The pace is slower, though the drums don't cease in volume or scope.  Vocally, Springsteen sounds `depressed' to exemplify and epitomize the lyrics and the title of the cut.  The sound of \\"This Depression,\\" like all other cuts, is well produced and crafted.  Title track \\"Wrecking Ball,\\" like the six preceding cuts does not disappoint, yielding yet another noteworthy addition.  The optimism of \\"Wrecking Ball\\" helps to make it so enjoyable, given its thudding percussive groove, which enters upon the second verse, not to mention those well orchestrated strings and horns. Bruce sings with conviction \\"Bring on your wrecking ball/bring on your wrecking ball/c'mon and take your best shot/let me see what you got/bring on your wrecking ball...\\" Seven for seven.<br /><br />\\"You've Got It\\" continues to compel, again evolving into full fruition with horns and clearly produced Springsteen vocals.  \\"Rocky Ground\\" may be less compelling than some of the best, but loses very little momentum if any.  Michelle Moore is featured on vocals here.  The cut opens with an ominous synth sound at the beginning. Here, Bruce strips the production for the first time, contrasting the production heaviness of previous cuts.  The songwriting remains solid and the use of choir is brilliant.  \\"Land of Hope and Dreams\\" may be lengthy at just under seven minutes, but it is one Wrecking Ball's best.  Featuring a beautiful saxophone solo by the late great Clarence Clemons and showcasing Bruce at his best where songwriting and arranging are concerned, \\"Land of Hope and Dreams\\" is a treat as the album's penultimate cut.  Closer \\"We Are Alive\\" has a lot of competition in its predecessor, and while it doesn't quite pull of the `upset,' it is appropriately placed and features the same sound orchestrations and instrumental portions as other cuts do.<br /><br />Overall, [[ASIN:B007F39498 Wrecking Ball]] is Bruce's strongest and perhaps most relevant release in years.  Well produced, well written, and well conceived, there are many more stronger points than weaker ones. No overt misses and a perfect duration of just over fifty minutes makes Wrecking Ball valedictory and a sound addition to any music lover's collection.he production heaviness of previous cuts. 0439023521|5.0|I don't normally go for this genre (futuristic, sci-fi), but succumbed to all the media hype and the recommendations of some trusted friends. And wow - did it live up to the hype. This book is a page turner from the very start, when you meet young Katniss Everdeen and the sad world she lives in. Her story takes place in a future state - in land once occupied by North America, but now designated as the nation of Panem, which rules over its twelve districts with a heavy hand (incidentally, \\"Panem\\" is derived from the Latin \\"panem et circenses\\" or, bread and circuses: provision of the means of life and recreation by government to appease discontent). The irony here is that Panem keeps its citizens down by keeping them in poverty and always hungry. And as punishment for and a reminder of a failed coup attempt against the Capitol, each district must surrender one boy and one girl to participate in  The Hunger Games each year, a fight to the death broadcast on live TV.<br /><br />I assumed it would be a violent, graphic novel based on this premise, but was pleasantly surprised to discover a story more focused on survival, strategic thinking, and human compassion. Collins actually provides some underlying social commentary on pop culture's fascination with absurd reality TV programming, our reduced sensitivity to violence and war, and the real impact of material possessions - including food - on the haves and have-nots. A great book to analyze in that high-school or College English class!<br /><br />But putting analysis aside, The Hunger Games is a wonderfully entertaining story about a girl coming of age in a complicated world, learning to survive - quite literally, but also figuratively - and you'll find yourself rooting for her every step of the way (and brainstorming ways to get her out of the situation she's in!). I can't wait to read Book 2, and of course see the movie that came out today - I imagine Hollywood had a field day with the costumes and special effects forthis one (just hope they didn't make it too gory). And I predict Katniss will make it onto the top 100 Baby Names for 2012/2013 list ;)

0312641893|5.0|I listened to this book on my Ipod and I have to say I absolutely loved it. While the ending was a bit predictable, it didn't detract at all from the exceptional quality of Marissa Meyer's writing and the overall experience of this engaging story. This may be a twist on the Cinderella fairytale (and there are elements from other fairytales, too), but the similarities really are minor. Cinder is a completely unique concept: a futuristic science fiction story about a cyborg mechanic, a worldwide pandemic, tense relations between Earthens and Lunars, and, of course, a gorgeous prince. Not since Ella Enchanted by Gail Carsen Levine has a fairytale so completely captured the hearts of its readers or deserved so many accolades.<br /><br />In addition, I have to mention that this story is set in future China and that its characters are therefore Chinese. This fact is not heavy-handed at all. Nowhere are their Asian features described. We only make that inference from the setting, cultural landmarks and clothing, and their names. But I think in this day and age when the need for more cultural and racial diversity in books is a growing topic of interest, Cinder should stand as an example for others to follow. I applaud Ms. Meyer for reaching beyond our white/American comfort zone and creating a multi-cultural story we all can enjoy.<br /><br />A word about the age appropriateness of this story.  Cinder is considered YA, for ages 12 to 18, probably because the characters are in their late teens. But I found nothing in this book that would be inappropriate for younger audiences. Rather, the story and the writing itself is fine for middle grade readers. There is a single kiss and very minor violence involving a gunshot, but otherwise I plan to hand this to my nine and eleven-year-old kids as soon as I get my hands on a print version. This book is worth adding to my permanent home library.<br /><br />Oh, and by the way, according to Ms. Meyer's blog, the sequel, Scarlett, is forthcoming. Can't wait.

B00475AYJQ|3.0|If you're only interested in prose, you will enjoy this book.  Patrick Rothfuss poetically describes scene after scene while characters engage in witty banter.  If story progression and epic fantasy, are important to you, you should look elsewhere.  I loved Name of the Wind, but this book seemed stumble all over itself.<br /><br />Writing Style:  Awesome!<br />As many have mentioned, there's something about Rothfuss's classic writing style that makes you want to keep turning pages.  There is some charm, wit, prose, poetry...  And after reading this book, you'll probably look at things in the real world a bit differently.  His metaphors are enlightening.  His dialogue is entertaining.  His declarations are wise.  His songs are poetic.  This is the ultimate strength of the series.  Many parts of this book feel like classic literature and that's no small feat.<br /><br />Story:  Fail!<br />Unfortunately, if you're looking for a great story with momentum, it's not here.  This book reads like an American Pie film.  Think I'm crazy?  Let me summarize the first 650 pages for you.  A guy is in college, hanging with his buddies, and playing jokes on people.  He takes a bit of time off to study abroad and sleeps with a hot foreign chick.  He returns as a stud and his newfound sexual prowess makes him a hit with all of the ladies.  I kept waiting for Eugene Levy to show up and give an awkward description about how to use a condom correctly or Stiffler to make an ass out of himself.<br /><br />This is a book about Kvothe telling a scribe a story about his youth.  Unfortunately, Kvothe's youth is full of other people telling him stories.  So, this ends up being a book about a guy telling stories about other people telling stories.  And in some cases, it's about a guy telling stories about people telling stories about other people telling stories.  The worst part about this is that he skips over all of the major events of his life.  There's a trial that nearly gets him expelled from the university, that is summarized in a few sentences.  There's also a pirate attack, a shipwreck, being stranded at sea, that is briefly mentioned.  But every time he sits in a tavern with his buddies, everyone's outfits and meals are described to the fullest detail.<br /><br />In the Name of the Wind, Kvothe was driven, but outside factors kept thwarting his investigation of the mysterious Chandrian; this made him endearing.  In this book, Kvothe's own character flaws and arrogance seem to thwart his investigation.  He will blurt out something at the worst time or do something to greatly offend the wrong person.  This belittles Kvothe's quest to track down the Chandrian.  If the guy can't keep his emotions in check for a few weeks to get some new intel, then this Chandrian thing doesn't seem all that important.  Kvothe should just give it up, be a lute rockstar, and have crazy sex with all the girls on campus.  He just seems like a pompous jerk.  This book is really missing an antagonist.  But then you realize that the main antagonist in the book is Kvothe.<br /><br />For 1000 pages, there is also very little story progression.  Only 10% of the book actually advances the story.<br /><br />Characters:  Good<br />The characters feel like real people, and not medieval archetypes.  This is refreshing... for a while... They're entertaining and the dialogue is fun.  The professors are all quirky, but helpful.  Kvothe's friends develop (off screen).  There are some mercenary characters that are a breath of fresh air.<br /><br />Action:  Poor<br />When there is action, Kvothe seems more powerful than Gandalf and Jackie Chan combined.  It just feels wrong.  And many of the scenes that you want to read about are glossed over or described in faux martial arts terms.  Kvothe executes turning wheel followed by sparrow's beak, but his attack is parried by tiger's breath.<br /><br />World Building:  Interesting<br />A new warrior race is introduced to the story and their customs, beliefs, language, hierarchy, and history and completely developed in great detail.  This part of the book is very well done.  Kvothe also becomes the sex slave of a fae creature and her realm, history, beliefs, are also well thought out.<br /><br />Maturity:  Adults<br />The book begins with Kvothe in college, but the whole second half of the book is full of sex and graphic violence.  Kvothe doesn't even wear pants for a month.  Not kidding.<br /><br />Overall:  Mixed<br />You will devour this book if you want hundreds of pages of tavern scenes with a classic and entertaining writing style.  And if you can appreciate a fully developed fantasy race, you will enjoy that part as well.  But if you're looking for a story with momentum, this isn't it.  One major problem with the Kvothe telling his life to a scribe is that you can only get Kvothe's perspective, so all of the other characters feel like props.  If it was a wise sage telling the story of Kvothe, then he could get into Kvothe's life and all of the other characters.  OR if this was simply an epic adventure told as it was happening, it would have more momentum and you would actually start rooting and cheering for Kvothe as he struggles on his quest.  But in the end, this just feels like Kvothe telling a scribe a story about how he told everyone how awesome he was back in his college days.<br /><br />Read this book if you like poetic writing and wit.<br />Read this book if you want a fantasy novel that reads like literature.<br />Avoid this book if pacing and momentum are important to you.<br />Avoid this book if you want a fast paced adventure.

0307477479|3.0|This novel shares a characteristic with another recent novel, The Imperfectionists.  They both deal with a rotating cast of characters, interrelated through some thematic thread. They both unfold in a series of chapters that could be viewed as self-contained stories or, as I likened the segments of The Imperfectionists, episodes in a mini-series.<br />In The Imperfectionists, the unifying thread was an international newspaper based in Rome. With Goon Squad it is a cast of characters that have six degrees or less of separation from the music industry, specifically the punk movement of the 70's. Rock and roll seems to me to have always been difficult to depict in fiction without resorting to clich. For one thing, aspiring as well as successful musicians often conduct their careers through a series of clichs and it is easy to convey the flavor of the music business without delving terribly deeply into a depiction of characters that are more than representative types.  Egan is more successful when she takes the characters out of the rock and roll setting and sets them off on winding paths throughout their subsequent lives. Probably the characters that are the most memorable, partly because they appear more frequently than any others, are Sasha and her one-time employer, failed punk rocker turned music mogul Bennie Salazar.<br />She is largely successful at getting inside the minds of characters who all have dark sides. From the kleptomaniac to the would be rapist to the suicidal depressive she places the reader inside their minds.  Depicting the characters at different stages of their lives also helps flesh out their personalities. At certain points I was infuriated by the overreaching cleverness in episodes that seemed to call far too much attention to themselves. However, as the novel progressed I understood connections between characters and realized the scope of what she was trying to accomplish. She certainly doesn't lack ambition. The time frame spans the 70'sto the 2020's, meaning that she embarks on the territory of speculative fiction in the future segments. The brother's obsession with pauses in songs points to the theme of broken threads through time that is itself a continuous thread in this novel.  The depiction of a future culture where tastes are largely determined by preverbal toddlers who `text message' their wants is fascinating, even if it lacks plausibility. The changes to mass communication brought about by the evolution of language are carried to logical extremes although, again, I suspect that the cultural climate she presents in 2020-something is also fairly implausible.<br />One of Egan's major themes, that time takes us down several winding paths in unexpected directions and changes us in ways we could never have predicted, is nothing new. The scrambled structure conveys this theme more provocatively than a linear, chronological sequence would have done. Although I can understand the justification of the time-hopping structure I feel that I only caught brief glimpses of these characters and couldn't get to know them as well as I could if I'd spent more than two successive chapters with them. Except for Sasha and Bernie, I had to recalibrate and reconfigure characters in my mind with each subsequent chapter. Perhaps this proves that Egan succeeded in doing what she set out to do. Perhaps a second reading would confirm that. However, since I don't think it warrants a second reading any time soon, this impression will have to suffice. If I were grading the novel, I'd give it an A for ambition, B- for execution.

B007M6P01O|4.0|This is a great little series IF you love getting the crap scared out of you time and time again and are tired of shows that always end on a happy fuzzy note. It's a terrible series if you value intelligent, thought-provoking dialogue and characters that don't stretch the boundaries of believability time and time again until you find yourself shouting at the zombie, \\"Eat her already, I'm tired of her whining. And while you're all at it, pick up that holier than thou sheriff and his wife too would you????\\" I really did try to dislike this series on that basis alone, really I did since I found the characters annoying and incredibly stupid in ways that defy even the most basic logic. For instance, Season 2 opens with our band of heroes (such as they are) stranded on an open highway. Now, they've been dealing with the walking undead for an entire season already. They should know this is a very, very bad situation. And yet, they wander around poking into vehicles while talking loudly acting as if it's just another Sunday afternoon at the park. When the zombies show up, as they do (they ALWAYS do) our little band is woefully unprepared for them. YET AGAIN. What happens from there could be called a comedy of errors, but I didn't find myself laughing. Instead, I kept reaching for the off button finding myself increasingly irritated and annoyed with the lot of them. But, like a train wreck or that bad auto accident that one passes on the freeway I found myself unable to look away. So I kept watching. I have to say as zombie shows go this one rocks, the walking undead are fascinating characters all by themselves, to heck with the human survivors. And who doesn't enjoy a good zombie show just for the vicarious thrill of seeing some undead former person slowly and implacably snarling and lunging forward determined to do one thing and one thing only, snack on living flesh! And oh the joy of screaming, \\"Run already you idiots!\\" as you get that first glimpse of a band of zombies around the corner knowing that the people are going to blunder right into the middle of them.<br /><br />Besides I love a good survive against all odds TV show or movie even if I do tell myself time and time again, \\"I would so rock that situation since I'm way smarter than any of these people.\\" And then I realize that part of the point of this series is that no, maybe I wouldn't be that smart. I might just do as many dumb, clueless things as its characters do. Why? Because they are human after all, ordinary people that may have been nice enough folks as you passed them on your way to a Sunday afternoon barbecue back when the world was normal. And yet, just like you they find themselves woefully, stupidly unprepared to survive in this new human eat human world. So do what I did if you're watching this series. Grab a good pillow or blankie to hold up to your face during the grosser moments, make peace with the fact that the human survivors are going to do dumb things repeatedly and cheer like mad for them and the zombies and place your bets on who wins with your friends. The one thing this show isn't is dull and boring and predictable. EVER. And in the end that's what makes this a great series after all in spite of its flaws. The fresh and the unexpected will draw you back time and time again.  Heck, right now I'm so hooked I have to admit I'd actually watch reality TV if it had zombies in it. \\"Snookie, drop that dress and run. There's a zombie shuffling up the aisle straight towards you!\\" So yeah, even though I keep telling myself I won't watch Season 3 when it comes out the sad truth is I probably will, at least until or if it gets boring and predictable.<br /><br />Let's face it folks, The Walking Dead is one of life's guilty pleasures.

B00466HN72|5.0|Honestly, there are so many great things about it! It's a fine film for any age, with plenty of humor that is genuinely funny for adults as well as kids, and the adult humor is not inappropriate for the younger set. The animation is fantastic and fully believable in the real-world, the pacing is some of the best I've seen, and the human characters are beautifully cast and all excellently played as well. There is no forced romance with an unnecessary love interest, because they do the right thing (for once) and don't have an unnecessary love interest! Yes, this movie is totally safe for the lovelorn or those who just don't like seeing unnecessary romances in movies that aren't about that! What IS it about? It's an Easter movie! Yes, an actual Easter movie that isn't religious. Don't get me wrong, I can appreciate the religious Easter movies too, even if I'm not so religious anymore, but it's nice to have something to watch on Easter (feature-length, I mean) that gives us a break from the religious stuff and still is about the holiday. If you have kids, this movie should be a real Easter treat for them, but I don't have kids and still LOVE it. It's just so much fun, I can really relate to the lead human character (A LOT), and the Pink Berets, they are just too adorable! I want to hug them to death (well, maybe not to death), especially the little one, Bit! I had to get the figurines of all three of them!<br /><br />But, I'm getting ahead of myself here, let me give a little on the plot. \\"Hop\\" is a film about E.B. (Russell Brand), the son of the current Easter Bunny (Hugh Laurie), who would rather drum in a band than follow in his father's paw-prints. It's also about Fred O'Hare (James Marsden), a directionless young man who is being pushed by his family to find a job and get his own place, but who just can't stand to settle into a life that isn't amazing. Lucky for both Fred and E.B. that their paths cross when E.B. runs away from their Wonka-esque candy factory on Easter Island to pursue his dream in Hollywood! Unfortunately for E.B., his father sends his elite royal guards, the Pink Berets (three cuter than cute girl bunnies) to retrieve him, and even worse, the Easter Bunny's number one chick, Carlos, decides now is the perfect time to stage a takeover and become the new Easter... Chick!<br /><br />There's also one other huge aspect of the plot that I normally wouldn't give away, because it would normally be a huge spoiler. The big, surprise ending. However, if this film has any flaws, it's that this potential surprise ending is revealed at the beginning of the film, literally in the opening! Yeah, just like in the original Salem's Lot. I can't for the life of me understand why some films do this. So, it's no spoiler to say that in the end of the film, Fred becomes the new (and first human) Easter Bunny. Honestly, even when he comes up with the idea and E.B. starts training him in the hopes that they can switch places, so to speak, it still seems unlikely that things will turn out with Fred as the new Easter Bunny, but they don't get to surprise us with that great turnout because they revealed it in the opening credits narration. Oh well. So, yeah, this film isn't perfect, but that one, pretty silly move on the filmmakers' part is the only glaring mistake. Having said that, I do love the idea of how things worked out for Fred since it was done in a way to even satisfy those of us who were a little concerned about not having a rabbit in that sacred position.<br /><br />Now, some might say they should have at least given one nod to the religious aspects of Easter, and I'll agree that some sort of allusion could have done just a bit more to make this a perfect Easter film that doesn't miss a beat, but I can live with it being wholly secular since almost every other feature film related to Easter is wholly religious. Something that bothered me a little bit more (and, again, not much, and even less upon repeat viewings) were little Christmas similarities. Most significantly, the fact that the Easter Bunny drives a flying sleigh in this film (it looks very Easter-ish and is pulled by a flock of chicks, but still...). If they hadn't made a joke by calling out the chicks' names, Santa-style, at the end of the film, it wouldn't have bugged me so much. It just would have been nice to avoid any Christmas references in this Easter film (I love Christmas, but do the other holidays always have to bow down to it? I also get bugged when the other monsters are made to bow down to Dracula), but nevertheless, it is a fantastic film! Gotta watch this one once a year at the very least! Definitely a new favorite of mine!<br /><br />The Blu-Ray/DVD/Digital Copy combo pack gives perfect picture and sound, and a handful of extras. The most boasted of is an all-new Mini-Movie. Now, I highly recommend picking this up, because the movie is so fantastic and also because there aren't many movies out there for Easter anyway, even when you include holiday specials (my picks in that area being Charlie Brown and Rankin/Bass's original Here Comes Peter Cottontail). But, don't be swayed over the Mini Movie. That was lame. I mean, it was not like one of those bonus cartoons PIXAR adds to its releases. This was basically just Carlos and Phil, the two chicks from the film (and, all through this review, when I say chicks, I mean baby chickens, though these chicks are adults that permanently look like chicks), dancing on a while background. Seriously. It was like an animation test, really, not a mini movie. I was hoping it might be a new Pink Berets adventure, but no such luck. It was no kind of adventure at all, and Hank Azaria didn't even provide the voices this time. I could tell right away that Carlos was played by Jim Cummings in this short and elsewhere on the disc. The other bonus features were a mixed bag too. There is a collection of behind the scenes interviews that should have just been presented as one featurettes, preferably with more material, but it was a nice inclusion anyway. I don't care much for DVD games, but they do have a trio of decent ones for the kiddies here. There are also some special Blu-Ray enhancement gimmicks of the kind I usually don't bother with (BDLive and all that sort of thing). Too bad movies like this rarely get commentary tracks, and I didn't see the theatrical trailer or anything of that nature on the disc. So, yeah, the bonus features aren't much, but the movie is a must own if you have any love for Easter at all.e Christmas similarities. B005K0HDGE|3.0|Stephen King is pretty much review-proof, so I won't spend a lot of words running down the aspects of this novel that didn't work for me. The high average ranking shows that most readers were on his wavelength and didn't mind (or didn't register) the anachronistic elements that crept into his characters' speeches, not to mention the slapdash construction of a several scenes that really needed a rewrite or, better, a complete reconsideration of whether they added anything to the smooth progress of the set of interlinking stories told here. Time travel stories require a particular level of insight and sophistication that strike me as missing from this book. For a really good time travel story, check out Tim Powers' \\"The Anubis Gates.\\"<br /><br />I go back and forth on King, who at his best is America's Dickens (long complex tales told through a huge set of well realized characters) and at his worst American's Bulwer-Lytton (\\"It was a dark and stormy night.\\"), a tedious hack who slathers words onto the page with a trowel and creates characters with no more differentiation that a bunch of cliches. Before this outing, \\"The Dome\\" had put him back in my good graces by virtue of being a contained, focused story where tensions and pressures on the lead characters ratcheted right up to a will-they-survive-or-will-they-not conclusion. \\"11/22/63\\" goes in too many different directions and introduces some dramatic threads that are there just because there is a tradition of parallelism, foreshadowing, and recollection in American novels. Grade C for vision, and another C for realization. On the other hand, there are reflective stretches where King abandons his characters for a while and just discourses on ideas of fate, free will, and causality. Those are the best parts of the book. His characters are not at their best when they are speaking or doing things. But when King lets then reflect on the reality that they are experiencing, or reflects on it for them in a way that lets thereader assign the musings to this character or that one, the book starts to fly. King definitely has an \\"A\\" game in him; he just needs to let it come out more often -- or force it do so.<br /><br />There was no chance that this book would displace the best Oswald novel from its top spot: \\"Libra\\" by Don DeLillo. To be fair, King is not writing a simple Oswald novel, but the story line to this book demands that the assassin be a bigger, better realized character than he actually is in these pages.<br /><br />Rossini said of Wagner's operas that they had some beautiful moments and some terrible half-hours. The same sort of judgment can be applied to King in general and this novel in particular.

0609610570|4.0|Bossidy and Charan dive into the critical gap of getting things done in this management classic. Focused on execution, the authors discuss the critical components for leadership within an execution/get stuff done environment. Pointing out the failures of hands off management, ivory tower thinking and micromanagement, the emphasis is on those critical elements that combine the what needs to be done and who needs to do it to execute on a business strategy or operational plan.<br /><br />The critical linking of strategy, operations and people is the role of the business leader, and in order to accomplish that critical mission, the leader must be meaningfully involved in those three elements - not micromanaging them, but leading them to efficient execution. The leader's most important job is the selection and appraising of the people in the organization, and this element should consume the bulk of their efforts and should never be delegated. The leader must embrace this role and thrive in it to ensure proper execution of the strategy and operations.<br /><br />The combination of people and strategy lead to an operating plan, in which the execution takes place. This plan needs to be realistic, ambitious, resourced, specific and accountable. The authors note that this piece is often the art that falls short - where the operating plan is divorced from the strategy and people - and the execution suffers.<br /><br />The book has critical questions and suggestions for the leader, and provides a platform for thought that is focused on getting things done rather than just the what and who theory of planning. An important read.

And if you keep reading until the end of the series you would rejoice when that tyranny is ended-- while feeling sorrow at the huge cost of that victory. For hand-held shots, I just bought a pistol grip ($14.00) and a square flash bracket (that doubles as a Microphone stand - $10) and I'm getting very decent video without the pain, time and expense. These units seem to require a small dose of masochism on the operator's part. I'm returning mine.
